=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I love games in which you can benefit from other players' moves. If you have one survivor in a boat with two of an opponent's survivors, you can rely on that opponent to move the boat. Thus, your survivor moves without you having to use part of your turn, increasing your action economy. Also, you have a second person fighting to keep the whales, sharks, and sea monsters away from that boat.

Better still, have a boat with only TWO survivors: one of yours and one belonging to an opponent. You can both move the boat, so it can reach the shore more quickly than a boat controlled by only one person.

I love this sort of begrudging cooperation.

RATING: 7
To help randomise the positions of the island tiles and to prevent positional bias - we do the following: starting with all terrain tiles face down and shuffled, work in a spiral around the centre of the island and working round in a clockwise fashion increasing the spiral with each finished circle - roll 1d6 to give the following results: 1 or 2 = position a beach tile, 3 or 4 = position a forest tile, 5 or 6 = position a mountain tile.  if ever a terrain type is used up then use 1, 2, 3 for the lower level terrain and 4, 5,6 for the higher level terrain.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
*edit* I think I'm willing to admit that Survive is one of those games I have for other people, rather than for myself. There is something rather arbitrary about the game. That quality has its charm, but it also pulls some of the meaning from player decisions. It's still a fun game, but only really in the goofy "screw you" way. That's enough sometimes I suppose.

Survive is the perfect game for families who enjoy some friendly competition. The rules are very straightforward, but there is a lot of room for fun screwage and playful banter. There's not a lot of depth to the game, just lots of good times. It's not hard to see why the game was a mainstream hit in the 1980's. 

The reprint by Stronghold games is an absolute home-run. The components are all great. My only complaint is the language-independent tiles. A little cheat-sheet would have been nice.

RATING: 6
This game is pure, wonderful cruelty wrapped up in a fancy-looking package. The strategy in this game is somewhat muted - you basically scramble to shore, knocking your opponents over when you can - and yet what's there is constantly fun. There's even an element of negotiation as you form brittle alliances (often by jumping unannounced into other players' boats) and plead for someone to send the sharks somewhere else.

RATING: 6
Rated after one play (four players).  Shows definite promise.  Has some Tigris and Euphrates Syndrome, in that the amount of stuff that can happen between two of your turns makes planning difficult or impossible, which makes me suspect that it (like T&E) might be best with two players.  Also not sold on the whirlpools, which seem to add an unneeded element of crapshootery.  Finally, I don't see the point of the rule that you can't look at your pieces' values during the game - turns what's otherwise reasonably strategic into a game of memory.

Want to play again to develop further impressions.

RATING: 8
Great game of planning and backstabbing. It's one of those games where you can completely screw people over, but still fail miserably in your own strategy. This is one of those games that are a guaranteed success.

You backstab by running other player's people into the sea, eating them with sea monsters and capsizing their boats. At the same time, you have no idea of the value of those you rescue yourself. You are able to rescue the most in numbers but sacrifice your most valuable pieces to the depths of the sea, ultimately losing you the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Have played this a couple times:  once with 2 and once with 4.  4 is much better, not surprisingly.  As advertised, it's a casual, MEAN little family game where most of the fun comes from killing your opponents' meeples with sea serpents, sharks, and whirlpools.  I like the light social negotiation stuff that emerges naturally ("If you move that sea serpent away from me, I'll pick your swimmer up in my boat next turn", etc.) and if people get into the spirit of the game, it's a blast.  Definitely light and random, but that's nice too - anyone can win.

I've only played with the variant where you don't have to use the numbers under the meeples.  Using that rule would add unnecessary cognitive load, from my standpoint - but I'll try it one day.

RATING: 8.25
#58 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2016)
#31 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2015)

Survive does a great job of emulating theme. You really feel like you are helping your people flee from Atlantis as it slowly sinks. People seem to dislike the slight memory aspect of this game, but I've always been a fan of memory challenges, so I welcome it. This game certainly isn't filled in nail-biting decisions. But it's fairly light, quick fun. And more often than not, that's what I'm looking for.

RATING: 8
This game is great for kids! It's a simple and very visual game. It'd be helpful to have simple crib notes for everyone that list the four turn phases:

(1) Optional: Play 1 previously-drawn Tile (those with a hand symbol).
(2) Optional: Take up to three movement actions:
  (a) Move from a land space to a land space.
  (b) Move from a land space into an adjacent boat.
  (c) Move from a land space to a water space.
  (d) Move from a water space to a water space.
  (e) Enter a same-space boat from the water.
  (f) Move a boat.
  (g) Exit a boat into the same water space.
  (h) Exit a boat into an adjacent, corner land space.
  (i) Climb ashore onto one of the corner spaces.
  Note: Swimmers may only take one action.
(3) Choose one Tile to sink.
  (a) Follow the instructions on the back of the Tile.
  (b) Other players can play a defensive Tile.
(4) Roll the dice and move one sea creature.
  (a) Other players can play a defensive Tile.

RATING: 7.5
The first thing people said before playing was "looks like we're playing a game for three-year-olds." Then they got mardy when their meeples drowned and started behaving like three year olds, which I found kind of funny and kind of sad.

It's hilarious when you crush the serious players boat with all their meeples on it, then ask them to look at the cute game artwork and explain why they're having a tantrum.

It's also fun to team up and try to make short-term diplomatic relationships with friends so both your meeples can get to shore safely...and occasionally kamikaze your one low scoring meeple into a sea monster along with two of theirs.

RATING: 6.5
This is a pretty fun little game! I generally prefer heavier, less cutthroat games, but I can't deny that it can be fun gobbling up other people's little swimmers!

--- Summary ---
Setup: randomly place tiles in the indicated area of the board. Players take turns placing their meeples (marked with VP values 1-6; mostly 1s) on unique hexes of the island; they may not look at values again till end of game
Each turn:
1. Optionally, play one previously acquired tile
2. Spend 3 movement points moving your swimmers/boats (swimmers move max 1)
3. Remove a tile from the board (beaches, then forests, then mountains) and look at the underside; some are saved till later, some are played immediately (if volcano drawn, game ends immediately)
4. Roll die and move the matching creature

RATING: 7
Light game where you are trying to save you meeples by getting them off a sinking island and onto some safe islands on the corners of the board.  As tiles are removed from the island (as it sinks);, these tiles are the trigger for adding sea monsters, sharks, whales and dolphins to the board.  There are of course boats that you can use (safe from sharks) put invariably many (most) of your peeps will be trying to swim for it and you may not realize this but  sharks swim faster than meeples.  It is light and entertaining.  Nothing you want to mess with if you want a brain burner, but a good evening ender or a low-stress warm-up.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
What a muddle. Survive: Escape from Atlantis is the latest entry in a series which has seen half a dozen different versions with slightly different rules over 30 years (Survive, Escape from Atlantis etc.). The general principle is that players control Atlanteans trying to escape their sinking island. The waters contain various creatures which eat swimmers, destroy boats or both; they appear as the island sinks, one tile removed per turn, because each tile has an effect such as "a shark appears here". Atlanteans reaching the outer islands are worth points.

Notable variations include whether:

* Tiles are flat cardboard or overproduced plastic, which is relevant from a rules perspective as the plastic editions enforce a practical tile limit of 3 meeples.
* Atlanteans can flee to adjacent land tiles or always become swimmers.
* Boats are placed randomly or in fixed positions.
* Sea creatures have movement distance fixed, determined by die roll or by other method.
* "Dives" (basically creatures teleporting around the map) are handled by tile draw or die roll.
* Dolphins exist and how they work.
* Atlanteans have numbers on the bottom worth 1-6 points or are just worth 1 each.
* The game ends when the volcano tile is drawn, when Atlanteans escape, when the last tile is removed etc.

Which variant is best is a matter of some debate. My rating for this Stronghold edition reflects that I find Escape from Atlantis to be more elegant in its implementation (fewer types of tile). There is certainly an argument to be made that fixing sea monster movement to 1, for example, makes positioning more important because it is much harder for one to destroy a boat and eat all aboard. But tile effects are still wildly unbalanced and only conditionally useful so I can hardly credit that it is any less random.

Similarly, some argue that the hidden point values add a fun bluffing element but the game is so random that I don't think it stands up to scrutiny. I personally also prefer the end condition where all of one player's remaining Atlanteans reach an island, because it becomes possible to force an early game end by sending sharks after one's own swimmers.

I like the concept but not, in this latest version, the execution. Escape from Atlantis is a faster and more elegant game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
[Parker Brothers 'Survive' 82 Edition] {Nautical, survival; modular-board, dice rolling, secret unit; optimal 4p} Survive! is very good family game with strong survival theme.  It's a pity that it's no longer in print. It is a fun and strategic game. Players have to cleverly move their pawns to get high scores. They also have to decide when to let go some pawns.  It's very funny to watch serpents/sharks/whales moving towards swimmers or boats and exciting to know what event is going to happen. It's of strong player interaction and confrontation. In all, it's an exceptional family game. Every game collector should have a copy in their collection.

RATING: N/A
This is the game that got our family on to boardgames again. Growing up when boardgames were mentioned all I knew of were Parker Brothers or Hasbro games which never appealed to me. Then one summer, while picking my son up from summer camp, he was playing this with his friends. Each time I picked him up they were playing and it intrigued me. So I looked for it and finally bought it...then all the expansions! We were hooked. Then I started looking for more like it and discovered just how great and diverse some of these games were. Now I'm only limited by how much my wife will let me buy!

RATING: 7
It is surprising that a Parker Brothers game from the eighties can be so good.  Survive is an excellent game that has a little bit of a nasty side.  You commonly have to make a decision on who to go after with the sea monsters, and since victory points on the potential survivors are quite varied (from one to six) and hidden, you just have to go with a feel for who is in the lead.  This plays best with four, because in the four player game, you know going in that time is going to be extremely tight, and on almost everyone's turn another guy gets dunked in the sea.  A lot of meeples aren't going to make it to shore, which gives the game a nice tension and sense of forboding before you even start.  The cardboard bits are pretty thin, which detracts a little from the overall feel of the game, but it is fun.

RATING: 6.5
Fun, straight-forward game for a humorous family game night and scales very nicely down to two (just use two colors each). I really like the tiles and their dual-use as action cards (even the thickness varies among terrain types--a really cool detail). Gameplay can be a bit dull as a lot of times your choices are obvious, but the fun is much more in sabotaging your opponents than getting your own people safe. Overall, it's a very light, cutthroat game that's good at keeping everyone engaged. It requires very little thinking, so it's a particularly welcome diversion after a long day.

RATING: 8
This was another surprise hit. I wanted to try it for a while because it gets generally positive ratings and has great production value, but I always thought it maybe seemed too light and random. After playing it, I have to say the light and randomness is actually a strength here. Much like Cosmic Encounter of Blood Bowl Team Manager, things that are outside of your control are just going to happen. A sea monster will unexpectedly eat a boat full of your people. One of your opponents might suddenly jump out a boat that you were in together and then swim to safety with the help of a dolphin. A lifeboat might suddenly appear next to the last person you had still trapped on the island and now they can escape. This is a light game with a good amount of randomness, but it's a lot of fun.

RATING: 6
Great fun. It doesn't offer deep strategy--which I usually prefer--but does offer a great deal of attack opportunitites for all players. I recommend it for families who want a fun-themed, cut-throat experience.

1/8/12 -- I'm going to bump this down to a flat 7. It just doesn't have staying power. I'll keep it because I have certain members of my family who LOVE it. 

I also bought the 5-6-player mini expansion and I don't recommend that to anyone unless you absolutely need it. Adding the extra players add too much downtime between plays, add chaos to an already chaos-loaded experience. Also, it comes with terrible non-sticky stickers for the plastic meeples.

3/4/12 -- The more my family asks to play this game, the more I realize I don't like it. I'm bumping it down to a 6 flat. Again, I'll keep it because there are a couple younger members of my family who really like it. But I'll probably never bring up the idea to play it again.

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 3
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 1

RATING: 10
The Holy Grail of light games.

Considering that Survive was originally published in 1982,  it is s safe bet that this may be the number one game of those that are categorized as "way ahead of their time".

Regardless of when this was released,  be it decades ago or just yesterday,  this is a nearly flawless design that yields a level of fun unrivaled by all challengers.

I own both the original Parker Brothers edition and the Stronghold 30th Anniversary edition.  The original has a nostalgic charm,  with excellent components considering it's era - featuring, as Quint might say,  meeples that look like "they'd been bitten in half below the waist".

Stronghold upgraded the game,  which for the most part was befitting of this classic gem.  Some components,  like the wooden boats and thick tiles are obvious improvements.  Some are less so,  namely the use of the color black for the shark fin and the less than menacing look of the powder blue whales - not to mention the meeples who appear to walk on water.  Perhaps I am a bit harsh on the revamped edition - a lot of effort went into all aspects of the design - and it shows.  I suppose the fact that I hold this game in such high reverence has much to do with it.

Survive excels at the full player count of 4,  or as a 2 player game providing you use 2 colors each - which is my favorite way to play.  You need to start with a full island to visually appreciate the chaos that is sure to ensue.

All in all,  I recommend the stronghold edition over the original - if not for the sole reason that you will want to play this game repeatedly - and those components,  at the very least,  stand a better chance of survival.  You should be so lucky.......

RATING: 6
About [thing=2653][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=2653][/thing], like the description says, is a cutthroat game where players seek to evacuate their pieces from a island that is breaking up and sinking, while remembering where their highest-valued pieces are located to maximize their score. This it is. And it is also a game of revenge, persecution, some teamwork, backstabs and drowning, a good deal of drowning. Because most meeples will die. Boats will be destroyed, sharks will eat and sea serpents will crush and kill. Whatever the atlanteans did, it was probably really horrible, as the beasts can even teleport themselves in order to massacre more.

[thing=2653][/thing] is a fight to survive against nature, only in this case nature have human cunning on its side. 

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
In her turn the player have 3 movement points. She can move the same meeple, on land, one, two or three hexes. Or one, two hexes, and other, one hex. Whatever. If the meeple in swimming, it can only be move 1 hex - but multiple (up to three) meeples in the water can be moved 1 hex (or use 1 movement to climb into a boat). A boat can also me moved, but only if is empty or by the player(s) with the most meeples on it. If a meeple, swimming or using boat, reaches a beach, it is safe.

After its three movements, the player takes for herself one of the land tiles - first it must be a beach tile, once all of these were taken, then the forest tiles and, finally, the mountain tiles. Each tile has an action associated with it: the ones with green borders are used immediately, and usually serve to add things to the board (like sharks, whales, boats and sinking holes); the ones with red borders, that have varied uses, the player keeps and can be used in later turns.

Finally the player rolls the monster die, to know which monster she will be able to move. There are three types: whales, that move 0 to 3 spaces, and can destroy boats; sharks, that move 0 to 2 spaces, and can kill meeples on the water (but doesn't affect those on boats); and the sea serpents, that can only move 1 space, but do both things: destroy boats and kill people on the water (and can do both together, when destroying a boat with meeples). After moving one creature of the kind rolled on the die, the turn passes to the next player.

Play goes like this until the volcano tile (which is bellow one of the mountain tiles) is revealed. The game ends immediately and the winner will be the player that scores the highest among the point value of the meeples saved.

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
Where to move. With whom divide a boat with. Which sea animal to move. Which tile to take and, if is a red one, when to use it later. Good positioning and some teamwork can help a player a lot, as you can work together so save meeples, but you might have you 6 (highest value) on the boat, while your opponent has her 1 (lowest), so, while you both achieve the same goal: saving your meeple, you took greater advantage - and you can still use this as a bargain chip later "we worked so well to save these, we can do it again" or "I helped you here, don't kill my meeple!".

You can even use your value 1 meeples to cause havoc, entering with them on the first boats leaving the island (usually filled with the highest value meeple of others), only to be able to steer them to destruction. Don't expect much friendship later, though - but, there isn't much left anyway.

[thing=2653][/thing] is mostly tatical in nature, and players are required to do the best possible with what is given and what is going on. There are a lot of luck involved in the game, so there is little reason to overthink actions and options. Decide just what is safer and/or more fun.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Light and easy to teach;
- Lots of interaction between players;
- Good production value - the pieces are well done, the tiles are thick and the board is pretty;
- While there is high level of luck in the draw of tiles and in the die, most of the game is driven by players decisions, not random.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- A metric ton of take that - and is not even "guided". Normally, if a player has a chance to not screw anyone or to screw a player losing badly, the second option will most likely be taken 80% of the time. This happens because of the secrecy of the value of the meeples and works in two ways: I could have saved only two, but with them being my 5 and 6, while you saved 5, a 1, 1, 2, 3 and 4. We both have 11 points, but who do you think will take more heat? On the other hand, I could have saved my 1 and 3, but someone could think I did saved my 5 and 6, so I couldn't really count on mercy;
- Huge difference of usefulness of tiles: I can reveal a Boat tile that don't even help me at all, or a Swirl that can kill my meeples, while you can take a superb "Teleport the Sea Serpent to Anywhere", which is totally amazing and carries no drawback. By blind luck, one can do win (or lose) the game with the tiles taken;
- Due to the way the game works, someone could lose early all of her meeples and has no chance of winning, just staying on the game causing havoc.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
[thing=2653][/thing] reminds me of a chocolate cigarette: a relic of a past time, in which family oriented games where filled with things today we consider almost crazy. Today chocolate should have low sugar and no trans fat (I can't say for a fact, but I do believe these two items counted for the composition of 85% of the chocolates I ate in my younger years), and most definitely must not have a cigarette shape. For games, this means: family games now should have low harmful interaction, normally without killing and being eaten by sharks after being throwed in the water by Dad. This is the stuff psychologists thrive on.

While keeping itself simple and easy enough to understand and play, the game is filled with opportunities for everyone being mad at each other. It is all for fun, though. One can't really take anything in [thing=2653][/thing] seriously. It isn't a thinky game, nor it poses as one. Players have control over their movements, but so much can happen between your plays that you can only hope that something is on place when you are up again.

Of course, when playing with the kids, is possible to hold back and put mom and dad as targets and going around chasing air with the sea beasts. But best of luck trying to hold back an older sibling - still, the same could be said to the brawls started with my brother and friends due to Super Street Fighter II.

[thing=2653][/thing] relies on players to keep being mean and unforgiven - and the designers, being humans, knew it would work. Though the degree can vary.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Survive is a cutthroat game where players seek to evacuate their pieces from an island that is breaking up, while remembering where their highest-valued pieces are located to maximize their score.
An island made up of 40 hex-tiles is slowly sinking into the ocean (as the tiles are removed from the board). Each player controls ten people (valued from 1 to 6) that they try and move towards the safety of the surrounding islands before the main island finally blows up. Players can either swim or use boats to travel but must avoid sea serpents, whales and sharks on their way to safety.

RATING: 7
I had a copy of this during the 80's and it was one of my favorites at the time. The sinking island tile mechanic seemed pretty novel at the time and the use of action points to make your moves (and that of the sharks, whales, and sea monster when they come up on the special die) work well. Ahead of its time for a mass-market game from the 80s and the theme is extremely well integrated. Plus it plays quickly. I highly recommend playing a game if opportunity presents itself. A classic!

RATING: 8

I enjoyed some of the take that moments and the tension that develops during the game as more and more pieces come out to the board.  My wife and I both had fun and look forward to playing again soon.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This was the game that showed me the benefits of mean-spirited play.  It's not that you want to hurt the people you're playing against, but sometimes it's just in your best interest to hit them once or twice to gain an advantage.  I wouldn't recommend doing this to someone in real life, but in games, it's perfectly legitimate.  Survive! has some randomness through the tile draw and the die rolls, but hey, it wouldn't be much of a game without them!  Interestingly enough, this is one of those games that thrives off of the chaos, so it doesn't seem to click as well with just 2 players.  It's a little too easy for both players to do their own thing at that point, and it's far too easy to make plans that can carry from turn to turn.  I would mark this game as strictly a 4-player affair.

2 plays: 2 players
3 plays: 4 players

RATING: 7
One of the better mass-marketed games from most gamers' childhoods gets a well-deserved and well-made reprint.  The gameplay is identical to the original, and Stronghold made the right decision to include both versions of the game in the reprint.  It speaks a lot toward their commitment to making good games, and accommodating gamers from both sides of the pond.

SURVIVE!: Well, it's the same game, so I may as well copy my comments directly from there: This was the game that showed me the benefits of mean-spirited play.  It's not that you want to hurt the people you're playing against, but sometimes it's just in your best interest to hit them once or twice to gain an advantage.  I wouldn't recommend doing this to someone in real life, but in games, it's perfectly legitimate.  Survive! has some randomness through the tile draw and the die rolls, but hey, it wouldn't be much of a game without them!  Interestingly enough, this is one of those games that thrives off of the chaos, so it doesn't seem to click as well with just 2 players.  It's a little too easy for both players to do their own thing at that point, and it's far too easy to make plans that can carry from turn to turn.  I would mark this game as strictly a 4-player affair.

1 play: 2 players

RATING: N/A
Survive is a cutthroat game where players seek to evacuate their pieces from an island that is breaking up, while remembering where their highest-valued pieces are located to maximize their score.
\13\\13\
An island made up of 40 hex-tiles is slowly sinking into the ocean (as the tiles are removed from the board). Each player controls ten people (valued from 1 to 6) that they try and move towards the safety of the surrounding islands before the main island finally blows up. Players can either swim or use boats to travel but must avoid sea serpents, whales and sharks on their way to safety.

RATING: 6
I remember owning this as a kid and I played the hell out of it, often times solitaire. At some point, like most kids, I stopped playing because of some other new toy or distraction and Survive! was mostly forgotten.

Here we are a couple of decades later. My good friend Zxqueb is telling me about some old boardgame from his childhood he rediscovered while he was cleaning out his parent's attic. Survive!

The next games day, Zxqueb brought it over and it hit the table immediately. Amazingly enough, it was still as good as I remembered it as a kid. It was exciting to flip a tile and watch the island slowly disintegrate. It was fun to jump my guys into someone else's boat and take control from them. It was awesome to dump my opponents villagers into the drink...and the mouth of an awaiting shark. Even as I type this I am a little bit amazed that the game has held up so well. It has a remarkably modern feel for a game that is almost 30 years old. I really wish I had my old copy back...

UPDATE: Reprint....Woot!

RATING: 7
Survive is an excellent family game and yet it also has enough depth to be fun for a group of gamers as well.  Survive can be a pretty brutal and cut-throat experience (and that fierce competition is most of the fun) but as long as the players know that, it makes for a delightful time.

Highly recommended.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is Adrienne's favorite game. The objective of the game is to save as many of your colored meeples from the sinking island of Atlantis. Each player gets three movements, then the flip over a tile, and finally they get the chance to move a monster based on a die roll. Some of the tiles will put sharks or whales into the game which can eat swimmers or sink boats respectively. There are also sea monsters which can destroy anything it the same space as it. This is very much a "take that" type of game, and the more players the more meeple violence. It is very easy to learn and fairly quick to play, and in general a good game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I am surprisingly good at this game, which makes it all the more fun. I am good at remembering where my "maximum valued" pieces are and I seem to be good at whatever strategy this game entails, but I don't always like the fact that my pieces die (it can be aggravating when you're so close and then bang, shark attack!) and I don't always (read: never) remember what the shark, whales, and sea serpents do. In this case, I am surprisingly happy with trying to sabotage other players and can do so while keeping track of my own pieces, which is rare. I do get a sense of satisfaction when my pieces survive. Good theme, great execution, lots of fun.
PS. I also like the whole "who controls the boat" aspect and how tiles you flip can get you surprise bonuses or abilities. I enjoy that element of randomness. I don't like how evacuating a boat counts as a move and I always forget that part, d'oh! I don't like how random the end condition is. >

RATING: 8.5
This game is a classic. It proves this time and time again by being very new-gamer friendly. I love taking this game off the shelf in it's large square box and letting the box art and concept entice players. It also has an interesting competitive dynamic and memory friendly game play. 

I really like this game, but over time it becomes a repeat of the same mechanics same experiences and encounters. 

Update 10/4/2017:

When the average man thinks of board games they may recall sitting in doors on a rainy afternoon playing with their siblings. At some point some sibling does something reasonably diabolical in the game and another sibling gets annoyed or upset. Survive could very well be that game. When played as an adult it is a fun and nail-biting experience as everyone fends for themselves to survive a sinking island. This is a perfect gateway game and extremely easy to teach.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
Fun, fast and with enough strategy to make me happy.  Love the pieces--whales, sharks, sea serpents, dolphins, and the island sections.  

One of the reasons I enjoy gaming as much as I do is because of the tactile experience.  Touching, flipping, holding and tapping pieces as I contemplate my next move has always been enjoyable to me.

Survive: Escape from Atlantis has that element more than most games.  

Quick play, loaded with giggles, pleasant to look at.  Fine family game.  If you are attempting to lure your family into gaming, try Survive.  :)

RATING: 6.5
After playing a few sessions, this light beer & pretzels game turned out to be more enjoyable than originally expected.  Even though a player's choices are relatively simple, they could drastically affect victory conditions.  The randomness of the tiles certainly contributes to the unpredictability of the game and its replayability factors whilst helping to balance the varying experience levels of the players.

The components are extremely nice, well made and durable.  The board is especially beautiful and all the components reflect the theme quite nicely.  One would certainly feel he/she received his/her money's worth.

RATING: 6
For a lighter game, Survive is stellar. It is probably my favourite game that I could play with my parents. The rules are simple and easy to pick up, but there is enough depth of play to keep more strategy-oriented players interested.

A strength of this game is that play can be varied to different levels of competition. Both friendly and cutthroat games are possible, and I enjoy both experiences. A must-have for a family looking for boardgames.
